<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">
</td></tr>

<p><b>Sulfur dioxide</b> (also <b>sulphur dioxide</b> in <a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">British English</a>) is the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> with the formula <span class="chemf nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">S</a><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a><span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br /><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span></span>. It is a toxic <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> with a burnt match smell. It is released naturally by <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_activity" class="mw-redirect" title="Volcanic activity">volcanic activity</a> and is produced as a by-product of the burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> contaminated with sulfur compounds and copper extraction.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_and_bonding">Structure and bonding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Structure and bonding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>SO<sub>2</sub> is a bent molecule with <i>C</i><sub>2v</sub> <a href="/wiki/Point_groups_in_three_dimensions" title="Point groups in three dimensions">symmetry point group</a>.
A <a href="/wiki/Valence_bond_theory" title="Valence bond theory">valence bond theory</a> approach considering just <i>s</i> and <i>p</i> orbitals would describe the bonding in terms of <a href="/wiki/Resonance_(chemistry)" title="Resonance (chemistry)">resonance</a> between two resonance structures.
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg/350px-Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg.png" width="350" height="73" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg/525px-Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg/700px-Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="75" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sulfur-dioxide-resonance-2D.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two resonance structures of sulfur dioxide</div></div></div></div>
<p>The sulfur–oxygen bond has a <a href="/wiki/Bond_order" title="Bond order">bond order</a> of 1.5. There is support for this simple approach that does not invoke <i>d</i> orbital participation.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
In terms of <a href="/wiki/Electron_counting" title="Electron counting">electron-counting</a> formalism, the sulfur atom has an <a href="/wiki/Oxidation_state" title="Oxidation state">oxidation state</a> of +4 and a <a href="/wiki/Formal_charge" title="Formal charge">formal charge</a> of +1.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occurrence">Occurrence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Occurrence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Io_Aurorae_color.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Io_Aurorae_color.jpg/220px-Io_Aurorae_color.jpg" width="220" height="206" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Io_Aurorae_color.jpg/330px-Io_Aurorae_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/62/Io_Aurorae_color.jpg 2x" data-file-width="374" data-file-height="350" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Io_Aurorae_color.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The blue auroral glows of Io's upper atmosphere are caused by volcanic sulfur dioxide.</div></div></div>
<p>It is found on Earth and exists in very small concentrations and in the atmosphere at about 1 ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-OwenPickering1997_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OwenPickering1997-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaylorSimpson1987_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaylorSimpson1987-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="not clear where, when, how much variability in time or space (March 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>On other planets, it can be found in various concentrations, the most significant being the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Venus" title="Atmosphere of Venus">atmosphere of Venus</a>, where it is the third-most significant atmospheric gas at 150 ppm. There, it condenses to form clouds, and is a key component of chemical reactions in the planet's atmosphere and contributes to <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-MarcqBertaux2012_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MarcqBertaux2012-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> It has been implicated as a key agent in the warming of early <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, with estimates of concentrations in the lower atmosphere as high as 100 ppm,<sup id="cite_ref-HalevyZuber2007_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HalevyZuber2007-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> though it only exists in trace amounts. On both Venus and Mars, as on Earth, its primary source is thought to be volcanic. The <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Io" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmosphere of Io">atmosphere of Io</a> is 90% sulfur dioxide<sup id="cite_ref-IobookChap10_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IobookChap10-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> and trace amounts are thought to also exist in the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Jupiter" title="Atmosphere of Jupiter">atmosphere of Jupiter</a>.
</p><p>As an ice, it is thought to exist in abundance on the <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean moons</a>—as sublimating ice or frost on the trailing hemisphere of <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, a natural satellite of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a><sup id="cite_ref-CruikshankHowell1985_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CruikshankHowell1985-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and in the crust and mantle of <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, possibly also in liquid form and readily reacting with water.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is primarily produced for sulfuric acid manufacture (see <a href="/wiki/Contact_process" title="Contact process">contact process</a>). In the United States in 1979, 23.6 million tonnes (26,014,547 US short tons) of sulfur dioxide were used in this way, compared with 150 thousand tonnes (165,347 US short tons) used for other purposes. Most sulfur dioxide is produced by the combustion of elemental sulfur. Some sulfur dioxide is also produced by roasting <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a> and other sulfide ores in air.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

The product is sulfur dioxide (SO<sub>2</sub>) with some traces of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">sulfur trioxide</a> (SO<sub>3</sub>). The "smoke" that exits the gas washing bottle is, in fact, a sulfuric acid fog generated in a reaction.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Combustion_routes">Combustion routes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Combustion routes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is the product of the burning of <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a> or of burning materials that contain sulfur:
</p>
<dl><dd>S + O<sub>2</sub> → SO<sub>2</sub>, ΔH = −297 kJ/mol</dd></dl>
<p>To aid combustion, liquefied sulfur (140–150&#160;°C, 284-302&#160;°F) is sprayed through an atomizing nozzle to generate fine drops of sulfur with a large surface area. The reaction is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a>, and the combustion produces temperatures of 1000–1600&#160;°C, 1832-2912&#160;°F). The significant amount of heat produced is recovered by steam generation that can subsequently be converted to electricity.<sup id="cite_ref-Ullmann_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ullmann-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The combustion of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and organosulfur compounds proceeds similarly. For example:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 H<sub>2</sub>S + 3 O<sub>2</sub> → 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roasting_(metallurgy)" title="Roasting (metallurgy)">roasting</a> of sulfide ores such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrite" title="Pyrite">pyrite</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sphalerite" title="Sphalerite">sphalerite</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cinnabar" title="Cinnabar">cinnabar</a> (mercury sulfide) also releases SO<sub>2</sub>:<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd>4 FeS<sub>2</sub>  +  11 O<sub>2</sub>   →   2 Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>  +  8 SO<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>2 ZnS  +  3 O<sub>2</sub> →   2 ZnO  +  2 SO<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>HgS  + O<sub>2</sub>   →   Hg  +  SO<sub>2</sub></dd>
<dd>4 FeS + 7O<sub>2</sub> → 2 Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> + 4 SO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>A combination of these reactions is responsible for the largest source of sulfur dioxide, volcanic eruptions.  These events can release millions of tonnes of SO<sub>2</sub>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reduction_of_higher_oxides">Reduction of higher oxides</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reduction of higher oxides">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide can also be a byproduct in the manufacture of <a href="/wiki/Calcium_silicate" title="Calcium silicate">calcium silicate</a> <a href="/wiki/Cement" title="Cement">cement</a>; <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfate" title="Calcium sulfate">CaSO<sub>4</sub></a> is heated with <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coke</a> and sand in this process:
</p>
<dl><dd>2 CaSO<sub>4</sub> + 2 SiO<sub>2</sub> + C → 2 CaSiO<sub>3</sub> + 2 SO<sub>2</sub> + CO<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Until the 1970s, commercial quantities of sulfuric acid and cement were produced by this process in <a href="/wiki/Whitehaven" title="Whitehaven">Whitehaven</a>, England. Upon being mixed with <a href="/wiki/Shale" title="Shale">shale</a> or <a href="/wiki/Marl" title="Marl">marl</a>, and roasted, the sulfate liberated sulfur dioxide gas, used in sulfuric acid production, the reaction also produced calcium silicate, a precursor in cement production.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On a laboratory scale, the action of hot concentrated sulfuric acid on copper <a href="/wiki/Swarf" title="Swarf">turnings</a> produces sulfur dioxide.
</p>
<dl><dd>Cu  +  2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → CuSO<sub>4</sub>  +  SO<sub>2</sub>  +  2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="From_sulfite">From sulfite</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: From sulfite">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfite results from the reaction of aqueous base and sulfur dioxide.  The reverse reaction involves acidification of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_metabisulfite" title="Sodium metabisulfite">sodium metabisulfite</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>  +  Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>  →  2 SO<sub>2</sub>  +  Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>  + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Industrial_reactions">Industrial reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Industrial reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Treatment of basic solutions with sulfur dioxide affords sulfite salts (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Sodium_sulfite" title="Sodium sulfite">sodium sulfite</a>):
</p>
<dl><dd>SO<sub>2</sub> + 2 NaOH → Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>Featuring sulfur in the +4 oxidation state, sulfur dioxide is a <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reducing agent</a>.  It is oxidized by halogens to give the sulfuryl halides, such as <a href="/wiki/Sulfuryl_chloride" title="Sulfuryl chloride">sulfuryl chloride</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>SO<sub>2</sub> + Cl<sub>2</sub> → SO<sub>2</sub>Cl<sub>2</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is the <a href="/wiki/Oxidising_agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxidising agent">oxidising agent</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Claus_process" title="Claus process">Claus process</a>, which is conducted on a large scale in <a href="/wiki/Oil_refineries" class="mw-redirect" title="Oil refineries">oil refineries</a>.  Here, sulfur dioxide is reduced by hydrogen sulfide to give elemental sulfur:
</p>
<dl><dd>SO<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>S → 3 S + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd></dl>
<p>The sequential oxidation of sulfur dioxide followed by its hydration is used in the production of sulfuric acid.
</p>
<dl><dd>2 SO<sub>2</sub>  +  2 H<sub>2</sub>O  +  O<sub>2</sub>   →   2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Laboratory_reactions">Laboratory reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Laboratory reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is one of the few common acidic yet reducing gases. It turns moist litmus pink (being acidic), then white (due to its bleaching effect). It may be identified by bubbling it through a <a href="/wiki/Dichromate" class="mw-redirect" title="Dichromate">dichromate</a> solution, turning the solution from orange to green (Cr<sup>3+</sup> (aq)). It can also reduce ferric ions to ferrous.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Sulfur dioxide can react with certain 1,3-<a href="/wiki/Diene" title="Diene">dienes</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Cheletropic_reaction" title="Cheletropic reaction">cheletropic reaction</a> to form cyclic <a href="/wiki/Sulfone" title="Sulfone">sulfones</a>. This reaction is exploited on an industrial scale for the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Sulfolane" title="Sulfolane">sulfolane</a>, which is an important solvent in the petrochemical industry.
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/File:Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg" class="image"><img alt="Cheletropic reaction of butadiene with SO2.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg/150px-Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg.png" width="150" height="63" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg/225px-Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg/300px-Cheletropic_reaction_of_butadiene_with_SO2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="160" /></a></dd></dl>
<p>Sulfur dioxide can bind to metal ions as a <a href="/wiki/Ligand" title="Ligand">ligand</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Metal_sulfur_dioxide_complex" title="Metal sulfur dioxide complex">metal sulfur dioxide complexes</a>, typically where the transition metal is in oxidation state 0 or +1.  Many different bonding modes (geometries) are recognized, but in most cases, the ligand is monodentate, attached to the metal through sulfur, which can be either planar and pyramidal <a href="/wiki/Hapticity" title="Hapticity">η</a><sup>1</sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greenwood_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_to_sulfuric_acid">Precursor to sulfuric acid</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Precursor to sulfuric acid">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is an intermediate in the production of sulfuric acid, being converted to <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_trioxide" title="Sulfur trioxide">sulfur trioxide</a>, and then to <a href="/wiki/Oleum" title="Oleum">oleum</a>, which is made into sulfuric acid. Sulfur dioxide for this purpose is made when sulfur combines with oxygen. The method of converting sulfur dioxide to sulfuric acid is called the <a href="/wiki/Contact_process" title="Contact process">contact process</a>. Several billion kilograms are produced annually for this purpose.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_preservative">As a preservative</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: As a preservative">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is sometimes used as a <a href="/wiki/Preservative" title="Preservative">preservative</a> for dried <a href="/wiki/Apricot" title="Apricot">apricots</a>, dried <a href="/wiki/Common_fig" title="Common fig">figs</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Dried_fruit" title="Dried fruit">dried fruits</a>, owing to its <a href="/wiki/Antimicrobial" title="Antimicrobial">antimicrobial</a> properties, and is called <a href="/wiki/E_number" title="E number">E</a>220<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> when used in this way in Europe.  As a preservative, it maintains the colorful appearance of the fruit and prevents <a href="/wiki/Decomposition" title="Decomposition">rotting</a>. It is also added to sulfured <a href="/wiki/Molasses" title="Molasses">molasses</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_winemaking">In winemaking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In winemaking">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide was first used in <a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a> by the Romans, when they discovered that burning sulfur candles inside empty wine vessels keeps them fresh and free from vinegar smell.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is still an important compound in winemaking, and is measured in <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (<i>ppm</i>) in wine. It is present even in so-called unsulfurated wine at concentrations of up to 10&#160;mg/L.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> It serves as an <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant</a>, protecting wine from spoilage by bacteria and oxidation - a phenomenon that leads to the browning of the wine and a loss of cultivar specific flavors.<sup id="cite_ref-Jackson_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Guerrero_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guerrero-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Its antimicrobial action also helps minimize volatile acidity. Wines containing sulfur dioxide are typically labeled with "containing <a href="/wiki/Sulfite" title="Sulfite">sulfites</a>".
</p><p>Sulfur dioxide exists in wine in free and bound forms, and the combinations are referred to as total SO<sub>2</sub>. Binding, for instance to the carbonyl group of <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a>, varies with the wine in question. The free form exists in equilibrium between molecular SO<sub>2</sub> (as a dissolved gas) and bisulfite ion, which is in turn in equilibrium with sulfite ion. These equilibria depend on the pH of the wine. Lower pH shifts the equilibrium towards molecular (gaseous) SO<sub>2</sub>, which is the active form, while at higher pH more SO<sub>2</sub> is found in the inactive sulfite and bisulfite forms. The molecular SO<sub>2</sub> is active as an antimicrobial and antioxidant, and this is also the form which may be perceived as a pungent odor at high levels. Wines with total SO<sub>2</sub> concentrations below 10 ppm do not require "contains sulfites" on the label by US and EU laws.  The upper limit of total SO<sub>2</sub> allowed in wine in the US is 350 ppm; in the EU it is 160 ppm for red wines and 210 ppm for white and rosé wines. In low concentrations, SO<sub>2</sub> is mostly undetectable in wine, but at free SO<sub>2</sub> concentrations over 50 ppm, SO<sub>2</sub> becomes evident in the smell and taste of wine.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>SO<sub>2</sub> is also a very important compound in winery sanitation.  Wineries and equipment must be kept clean, and because bleach cannot be used in a winery due the risk of <a href="/wiki/Cork_taint" title="Cork taint">cork taint</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> a mixture of SO<sub>2</sub>, water, and citric acid is commonly used to clean and sanitize equipment. <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">Ozone</a> (O<sub>3</sub>) is now used extensively for sanitizing in wineries due to its efficacy, and because it does not affect the wine or most equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_reducing_agent">As a reducing agent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: As a reducing agent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is also a good <a href="/wiki/Reducing_agent" title="Reducing agent">reductant</a>. In the presence of water, sulfur dioxide is able to decolorize substances.  Specifically, it is a useful reducing <a href="/wiki/Bleach" title="Bleach">bleach</a> for <a href="/wiki/Paper" title="Paper">papers</a> and delicate materials such as clothes.  This bleaching effect normally does not last very long. <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">Oxygen</a> in the atmosphere reoxidizes the reduced dyes, restoring the color.  In municipal wastewater treatment, sulfur dioxide is used to treat chlorinated wastewater prior to release.  Sulfur dioxide reduces free and combined chlorine to <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfur dioxide is fairly soluble in water, and by both IR and Raman spectroscopy; the hypothetical sulfurous acid, H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>, is not present to any extent.  However, such solutions do show spectra of the hydrogen sulfite ion, HSO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>, by reaction with water, and it is in fact the actual reducing agent present:
</p>
<dl><dd>SO<sub>2</sub> + H<sub>2</sub>O ⇌ HSO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup> + H<sup>+</sup></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Biochemical_and_biomedical_roles">Biochemical and biomedical roles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Biochemical and biomedical roles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is toxic in large amounts.  It or its conjugate base bisulfite is produced biologically as an intermediate in both sulfate-reducing organisms and in sulfur-oxidizing bacteria, as well. The role of sulfur dioxide in mammalian biology is not yet well understood.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>  Sulfur dioxide blocks nerve signals from the <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_stretch_receptors" title="Pulmonary stretch receptors">pulmonary stretch receptors</a> and abolishes the <a href="/wiki/Hering%E2%80%93Breuer_reflex" title="Hering–Breuer reflex">Hering–Breuer inflation reflex</a>.
</p><p>It was shown that endogenous sulfur dioxide plays a role in diminishing an experimental <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lung</a> damage caused by <a href="/wiki/Oleic_acid" title="Oleic acid">oleic acid</a>. Endogenous sulfur dioxide lowered lipid peroxidation, free radical formation, oxidative stress and inflammation during an experimental lung damage. Conversely, a successful lung damage caused a significant lowering of endogenous sulfur dioxide production, and an increase in lipid peroxidation, free radical formation, oxidative stress and inflammation. Moreover, blockade of an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that produces endogenous SO<sub>2</sub> significantly increased the amount of lung tissue damage in the experiment. Conversely, adding <a href="/wiki/Acetylcysteine" title="Acetylcysteine">acetylcysteine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Glutathione" title="Glutathione">glutathione</a> to the rat diet increased the amount of endogenous SO<sub>2</sub> produced and decreased the lung damage, the free radical formation, oxidative stress, inflammation and apoptosis.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is considered that endogenous sulfur dioxide plays a significant physiological role in regulating <a href="/wiki/Cardiac" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiac">cardiac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessel</a> function, and aberrant or deficient sulfur dioxide metabolism can contribute to several different cardiovascular diseases, such as <a href="/wiki/Arterial_hypertension" class="mw-redirect" title="Arterial hypertension">arterial hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_arterial_hypertension" class="mw-redirect" title="Pulmonary arterial hypertension">pulmonary arterial hypertension</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stenocardia" class="mw-redirect" title="Stenocardia">stenocardia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was shown that in children with pulmonary arterial hypertension due to congenital heart diseases the level of <a href="/wiki/Homocysteine" title="Homocysteine">homocysteine</a> is higher and the level of endogenous sulfur dioxide is lower than in normal control children. Moreover, these biochemical parameters strongly correlated to the severity of pulmonary arterial hypertension. Authors considered homocysteine to be one of useful biochemical markers of disease severity and sulfur dioxide metabolism to be one of potential therapeutic targets in those patients.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Endogenous sulfur dioxide also has been shown to lower the <a href="/wiki/Cell_proliferation" class="mw-redirect" title="Cell proliferation">proliferation</a> rate of endothelial <a href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> cells in blood vessels, via lowering the <a href="/wiki/MAPK" class="mw-redirect" title="MAPK">MAPK</a> activity and activating <a href="/wiki/Adenylyl_cyclase" title="Adenylyl cyclase">adenylyl cyclase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_A" title="Protein kinase A">protein kinase A</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Smooth muscle cell proliferation is one of important mechanisms of hypertensive remodeling of blood vessels and their <a href="/wiki/Stenosis" title="Stenosis">stenosis</a>, so it is an important pathogenetic mechanism in arterial hypertension and atherosclerosis.
</p><p>Endogenous sulfur dioxide in low concentrations causes endothelium-dependent <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>. In higher concentrations it causes endothelium-independent vasodilation and has a negative inotropic effect on cardiac output function, thus effectively lowering blood pressure and myocardial oxygen consumption. The vasodilating and bronchodilating effects of sulfur dioxide are mediated via ATP-dependent <a href="/wiki/Calcium_channel" title="Calcium channel">calcium channels</a> and L-type ("dihydropyridine") calcium channels. Endogenous sulfur dioxide is also a potent antiinflammatory, antioxidant and cytoprotective agent. It lowers blood pressure and slows hypertensive remodeling of blood vessels, especially thickening of their intima. It also regulates lipid metabolism.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Endogenous sulfur dioxide also diminishes myocardial damage, caused by <a href="/wiki/Isoproterenol" class="mw-redirect" title="Isoproterenol">isoproterenol</a> <a href="/wiki/Adrenergic" title="Adrenergic">adrenergic</a> hyperstimulation, and strengthens the myocardial antioxidant defense reserve.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_refrigerant">As a refrigerant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: As a refrigerant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Being easily condensed and possessing a high <a href="/wiki/Heat_of_evaporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Heat of evaporation">heat of evaporation</a>, sulfur dioxide is a candidate material for refrigerants.  Prior to the development of <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbons</a>, sulfur dioxide was used as a <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerant</a> in <a href="/wiki/Refrigeration#Home_and_consumer_use" title="Refrigeration">home refrigerators</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_reagent_and_solvent_in_the_laboratory">As a reagent and solvent in the laboratory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: As a reagent and solvent in the laboratory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is a versatile inert solvent widely used for dissolving highly oxidizing salts.  It is also used occasionally as a source of the sulfonyl group in <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>.  Treatment of aryl <a href="/wiki/Diazonium_salt" class="mw-redirect" title="Diazonium salt">diazonium salts</a> with sulfur dioxide and cuprous chloride yields the corresponding aryl sulfonyl chloride, for example:<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dd><a href="/wiki/File:Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg" class="image"><img alt="Preparation of m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl chloride.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg/500px-Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg.png" width="500" height="97" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg/750px-Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg/1000px-Preparation_of_m-trifluoromethylbenzenesulfonyl_chloride.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="99" /></a></dd></dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proposed_use_in_climate_engineering">Proposed use in climate engineering</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Proposed use in climate engineering">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stratospheric_sulfate_aerosols_(geoengineering)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stratospheric sulfate aerosols (geoengineering)">Injections of sulfur dioxide in the stratosphere</a> has been proposed in <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a>. The cooling effect would be similar to what has been observed after the large explosive volcano eruption of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Pinatubo#Global_environmental_effects" title="Mount Pinatubo">Mount Pinatubo</a> in 1991. However this form of geoengineering would have uncertain regional consequences on rainfall patterns, for example in <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> regions.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_an_air_pollutant">As an air pollutant</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: As an air pollutant">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent,_glows_at_night.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent%2C_glows_at_night.jpg/220px-Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent%2C_glows_at_night.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent%2C_glows_at_night.jpg/330px-Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent%2C_glows_at_night.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent%2C_glows_at_night.jpg/440px-Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent%2C_glows_at_night.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1630" data-file-height="1109" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sulfur_dioxide_emissions_from_the_Halema%60uma%60u_vent,_glows_at_night.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A sulfur dioxide plume from the <a href="/wiki/Halemaumau_Crater" class="mw-redirect" title="Halemaumau Crater">Halemaʻumaʻu vent</a>, glows at night</div></div></div>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is a noticeable component in the atmosphere, especially following volcanic eruptions.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>  According to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> the amount of sulfur dioxide released in the U.S. per year was:
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png/220px-Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png" width="220" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png/330px-Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png/440px-Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png 2x" data-file-width="1707" data-file-height="1582" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Estimates_of_past_and_future_SO2_global_anthropogenic_emissions.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A collection of estimates of past and future anthropogenic global sulphur dioxide emissions. The Cofala et al. estimates are for sensitivity studies on SO<sub>2</sub> emission policies, CLE: Current Legislation, MFR: Maximum Feasible Reductions. RCPs (Representative Concentration Pathways) are used in CMIP5  simulations for latest (2013–2014) IPCC 5th assessment report.</div></div></div>
<table class="wikitable">

<td>1970
</td>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1997
</td>
<td>19,363,000 short tons (17.6&#160;Mt)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1998
</td>
<td>19,491,000 short tons (17.7&#160;Mt)
</td></tr>

<td>1999
</td>
<td>18,867,000 short tons (17.1&#160;Mt)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Sulfur dioxide is a major air pollutant and has significant impacts upon human health.<sup id="cite_ref-EPA_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>  In addition, the concentration of sulfur dioxide in the atmosphere can influence the habitat suitability for plant communities, as well as animal life.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Sulfur dioxide emissions are a precursor to <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> and atmospheric particulates. Due largely to the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">US</a> EPA’s <a href="/wiki/Acid_Rain_Program" title="Acid Rain Program">Acid Rain Program</a>, the U.S. has had a 33% decrease in emissions between 1983 and 2002.  This improvement resulted in part from <a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">flue-gas desulfurization</a>, a technology that enables SO<sub>2</sub> to be chemically bound in <a href="/wiki/Power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Power plant">power plants</a> burning sulfur-containing <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> or <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">oil</a>. In particular, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_oxide" title="Calcium oxide">calcium oxide (lime)</a> reacts with sulfur dioxide to form <a href="/wiki/Calcium_sulfite" title="Calcium sulfite">calcium sulfite</a>:
</p>
<dl><dd>CaO + SO<sub>2</sub> →  CaSO<sub>3</sub></dd></dl>
<p>Aerobic oxidation of the CaSO<sub>3</sub> gives CaSO<sub>4</sub>, <a href="/wiki/Anhydrite" title="Anhydrite">anhydrite</a>.  Most gypsum sold in Europe comes from flue-gas desulfurization.
</p><p>Sulfur can be removed from coal during burning by using limestone as a bed material in <a href="/wiki/Fluidized_bed_combustion" title="Fluidized bed combustion">fluidized bed combustion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sulfur can also be removed from fuels before burning, preventing formation of SO<sub>2</sub> when the fuel is burnt. The <a href="/wiki/Claus_process" title="Claus process">Claus process</a> is used in refineries to produce sulfur as a byproduct.  The <a href="/wiki/Stretford_process" title="Stretford process">Stretford process</a> has also been used to remove sulfur from fuel. <a href="/wiki/Redox" title="Redox">Redox</a> processes using iron oxides can also be used, for example, Lo-Cat<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> or Sulferox.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SulufrDioxide2017.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/SulufrDioxide2017.png/220px-SulufrDioxide2017.png" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/SulufrDioxide2017.png/330px-SulufrDioxide2017.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/SulufrDioxide2017.png/440px-SulufrDioxide2017.png 2x" data-file-width="1121" data-file-height="561" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SulufrDioxide2017.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sulfur dioxide in the world on April 15th, 2017. Note that sulfur dioxide moves through the atmosphere with prevailing winds and thus local sulfur dioxide distributions vary day to day with weather patterns and seasonality.</div></div></div>
<p>Fuel additives such as <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a> additives and magnesium carboxylate may be used in marine engines to lower the emission of sulfur dioxide gases into the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2006, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> was the world's largest sulfur dioxide polluter, with 2005 emissions estimated to be 25,490,000 short tons (23.1&#160;Mt). This amount represents a 27% increase since 2000, and is roughly comparable with U.S. emissions in 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Safety">Safety</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Safety">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg/220px-20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg/330px-20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg/440px-20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1020" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:20180519_USGS_Leilani_Estates_Hawaii_Volcanic_EruptionDSC_0411_medium.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">US Geological Survey</a> volunteer tests for sulfur dioxide after the <a href="/wiki/2018_lower_Puna_eruption" title="2018 lower Puna eruption">2018 lower Puna eruption</a></div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inhalation">Inhalation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Inhalation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Inhaling sulfur dioxide is associated with increased respiratory symptoms and disease, difficulty in breathing, and premature death.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, the <a href="/wiki/American_Conference_of_Governmental_Industrial_Hygienists" title="American Conference of Governmental Industrial Hygienists">American Conference of Governmental Industrial Hygienists</a> reduced the <a href="/wiki/Short-term_exposure_limit" title="Short-term exposure limit">short-term exposure limit</a> to 0.25 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_million" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per million">parts per million</a> (ppm). The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">OSHA</a> <a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">PEL</a> is currently set at 5 ppm (13 &#160;mg/m<sup>3</sup>) time-weighted average. <a href="/wiki/NIOSH" class="mw-redirect" title="NIOSH">NIOSH</a> has set the <a href="/wiki/IDLH" class="mw-redirect" title="IDLH">IDLH</a> at 100 ppm.<sup id="cite_ref-NIOSH_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NIOSH-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>  In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/EPA" class="mw-redirect" title="EPA">EPA</a> "revised the primary SO<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/NAAQS" class="mw-redirect" title="NAAQS">NAAQS</a> by establishing a new one-hour standard at a level of 75 <a href="/wiki/Parts_per_billion" class="mw-redirect" title="Parts per billion">parts per billion (ppb)</a>. EPA revoked the two existing primary standards because they would not provide additional public health protection given a one-hour standard at 75 ppb."<sup id="cite_ref-EPA_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EPA-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2011 <a href="/wiki/Systematic_review" title="Systematic review">systematic review</a> concluded that exposure to sulfur dioxide is associated with <a href="/wiki/Preterm_birth" title="Preterm birth">preterm birth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Shah-2011_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shah-2011-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ingestion">Ingestion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sulfur_dioxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Ingestion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Science_in_the_Public_Interest" title="Center for Science in the Public Interest">Center for Science in the Public Interest</a> lists the two food preservatives, sulfur dioxide and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bisulfite" title="Sodium bisulfite">sodium bisulfite</a>, as being safe for human consumption except for certain <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthmatic</a> individuals who may be sensitive to them, especially in large amounts.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>  Symptoms of sensitivity to <a href="/wiki/Sulfite#Health_risks" title="Sulfite">sulfiting</a> agents, including sulfur dioxide, manifest as potentially life-threatening trouble breathing within minutes of ingestion.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
